###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: In answer to RW - again (was: Sorts (revised)

_(reply depth: 36)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2004-07-14T13:34:15-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0407141234.114d71a2@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <40ef37b7.94194265@news.optonline.net> <fgVHc.10939$R36.8417@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40f08a0f.180824052@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407111102.59a80371@posting.google.com> <40f1aef9.4304718@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407121258.8a48ced@posting.google.com> <40f398d0.129723189@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407131151.39465d2c@posting.google.com> <40f46c5f.183890458@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >> SQL permits the compiler .. at compile time .. to submit the statement to
> >> the database engine for validation. 
> >
> >No it can't.
 
> It could and routinely did on ALL databases I worked on -- Oracle, Informix,
> Sybase and mainframe DB2. 

First of it wasn't the _compiler_ that did that.  If it was anything
it was the pre-compiler provided by the database vendor.  The compiler
wpuld have been unaware that SQL even existed.  In fact by the time
the compiler got the output from the pre-compiler it didn't.
 
> The Oracle precompiler has options SYNTAX to bypass SQL validation and SEMANTICS
> to use it. If the program contains embedded PL/SQL, SEMANTICS is mandatory. I
> consider it good practice to use SEMANTICS at least once after major changes. 

>It will tell you about misspelled table and column names.

""" ----------------------------------
Pro*COBOL gets the information for a semantic check from embedded
DECLARE TABLE statements or, if you specify the option USERID, by
connecting to Oracle and accessing the data dictionary. You need not
connect to Oracle if every table referenced in a data manipulation
statement or PL/SQL block is defined in a DECLARE TABLE statement.

If you connect to Oracle but some information cannot be found in the
data dictionary, you must use DECLARE TABLE statements to supply the
missing information. During precompilation, a DECLARE TABLE definition
overrides a data dictionary definition if they conflict.
 ------------------------------------------- """

It does _NOT_ "submit the statement to the database engine for
validation."
 
It does check, as far as it can, if it needs to, the names against the
dictionary.

> My experience was on all the best-selling databases. Your ad hominem is
> supplying bad information to innocent readers. You are telling them it cannot
> be done when, in fact, it is done routinely.

No, Robert, you were supplying bad information. It does _not_
"validate the statement by passing it to the db" at all.  It does
check the syntax (without passing to the db) and may check the names
in the db dictionary if they are not in a declare table, except for
those in dynamic statements.

> The current ANSI SQL Standard requires every database to be capable of
> PREPARE, which means 'compile this and tell me about any errors'. That's how
> precompilers verify SQL. 

You are confused.  Prepare is done at run-time.

At pre-compile time, even for non-dynamic SQL, the data item values
are unlikely to be known and so a PREPARE may not be able to give a
valid result.

But, even more so, if I were to have a program that created a
temporary table, used then deleted it then the SQL to do this need not
be in the source in any particular order as long as they execute in
the correct sequence.  The PREPARE may completely fail - it _cannot_
work (except in simplistic and naieve ways).

Or, for example if programs are being developed to amend a table and
add fields.  PREPAREs of statements accessing those fields would fail
until the database is actually updated.

> Lacking access to some database, you wouldn't be able to test the program you
> just compiled. 

There is no requirement, that I am aware of, that testing must be done
on the same system that the pre-compiler or compiler runs on.  I
don't, for example, run MS SQL.  I test against what I do run and then
upload to the client (who doesn't have the compiler) and test there.
 
> >One of which will fail if put to the wrong database.
> 
> In that case, you must use dynamic SQL. 

No, there is no _must_, it need not be dynamic SQL, it can be exactly
as written for the compiler that I indicated.  While the Oracle
pre-compiler may fail the syntax (without passing it to the DB) the
(pre-)compiler indicated will accept either, as I require, because the
choice of which to connect to is done at run-time.  It _can't_ pass it
to the DB because it doesn't know which one I will use.

> FWI, on Informix it's SELECT FIRST 10 ...

Exactly, there may be a need to have more IF statements.
 
> Dynamic SQL cannot be checked at compile time.

Exactly.  Those CANNOT "submit the statement to the database engine
for validation."

> My point was that the pre-processor should be integrated into the compiler.

Which vendor's pre-processor would you choose ?  Would one need to buy
a different compiler for each database system ?  What if you upgraded
to a new version of the database, would the compiler vendor have to
issue a new version to incorporate the new syntax checks ?

It seems to work very well as it does currently, what _advantage_
would there be in your suggestion ?

Of course, there is nothing stopping you write your own compiler with
this, or even taking OpenCOBOL and building whatever features you
want.

> The Standard would say the entire Data Division must be pushed to the
> *co-compiler*, not preprocessor. Thus it would see the 'whole program'.

Currently the pre-processors get to see the whole source and select
what they want, what actual difference, what advantage, it there in
having the two pushing code at each other while running simultaneously
?  What _benefit_ over the current sequential processing woulkd there
be ?
 

> If EXEC were in the Cobol Standard, there would be some mechanism for
> co-compilers to register themselves with Cobol.

The standard defines the _language_, not how the compilers interact
with each other, that is entirely an implementation issue, always has
been.

But what do you mean by 'register themselves with Cobol' ?  With the
standards committee ?  With the compiler vendor ?  Will it have to be
published in the standard ?

Or did you mean 'with the compiler' ?
```

```
>> PREPARE, which means 'compile this and tell me about any errors'. That's how
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>valid result.

Data item values don't matter. The statement selects a specific customer. It's
not necessary at compile time to know which customer.

>But, even more so, if I were to have a program that created a
>temporary table, used then deleted it then the SQL to do this need not
>be in the source in any particular order as long as they execute in
>the correct sequence.  The PREPARE may completely fail - it _cannot_
>work (except in simplistic and naieve ways).

Informix doesn't. Definition comes from source tables. INSERT INTO TEMP foo FROM
(SELECT * FROM tablename) gets its definition from source tables, so names would
already be in the dictionary. But others do require DECLARE (which effectively
discourages the use of temp tables).

I'm stumped. I don't know how a compiler/precompiler could handle that without
making two passes.

>Or, for example if programs are being developed to amend a table and
>add fields.  PREPAREs of statements accessing those fields would fail
>until the database is actually updated.

I'd set them up as an external ODBC data source.

>> My point was that the pre-processor should be integrated into the compiler.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>issue a new version to incorporate the new syntax checks ?

Whatever database(s) are 'registered'.

>It seems to work very well as it does currently, what _advantage_
>would there be in your suggestion ?

The interaction between compiler and database would be defined by an external
Standard rather than being ad hoc defined by the database provider. This would
make source code portable.

>> The Standard would say the entire Data Division must be pushed to the
>> *co-compiler*, not preprocessor. Thus it would see the 'whole program'.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>be ?

Again, portability and external controls.

>> If EXEC were in the Cobol Standard, there would be some mechanism for
>> co-compilers to register themselves with Cobol.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Or did you mean 'with the compiler' ?

Good question. In the past, language standards lived on islands that didn't
communicate with other islands. If they acknowledged interoperation, they would
have to define the communication. A new Standard independant of language would
have to define The Registry. 

I don't see that happening, so Never Mind.

I learned something here. The precompiler isn't using PREPARE, as I thought, it
is checking lexicon against ALL_OBJECTS.
```

```
invective'.

> Fujitsu and IBM have integrated SQL into the compiler.

Fujitsu does for a subset using ODBC (or unixODBC on Linux).  IBM does
for DB/2. It seems that if other databases are required then the
vendor's preprocessor would still be used.

> I've never seen DECLARE in application programs. That's an oddity. The check
> is against existing tables.

The check _might_be_ against existing tables.

> Dictionary? In Oracle, that would be the table ALL_OBJECTS. 

When I look at Oracle documentation it talks about the Dictionary.
 
> >> My experience was on all the best-selling databases. Your ad hominem is

> I've not seen applications do database administration, but it is
> hypothetically possible.

It is not just hypothetically possible, it is not particularly
unusual.  While you may be working with single machines in single
sites or where a specialist DBA does all the grubby bits, others are
distributing systems over many branches or clients where users can be
relied on to be users.

> I'd set them up as an external ODBC data source.

What you would or would not do on your sites is up to you (or your
superiors) but you wouldn't do it where the client is remote and
firewalled.

> >It seems to work very well as it does currently, what _advantage_
> >would there be in your suggestion ?
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> make source code portable.

would be improved by defining how the pre-processor and compiler
interract ?

In fact what part does 'interraction' play _at_all_ in the source code
?
 
> Again, portability and external controls.

Be specific, what is non-portable now that this interract would change
?  Which lines of source code would be different ?

> Good question. In the past, language standards lived on islands that didn't
> communicate with other islands. 

Actiually, in the past, they did.  Cobol had 'ENTER language' in the
early standards to allow other languages to be incorporated.  This is
obsolete in ANS'85.

Note that other forms of the ENTER also existed and were replaced by
CALL and the extension ENTRY.
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0407151830.53119503@posting.google.com...

> Actiually, in the past, they did.  Cobol had 'ENTER language' in the
> early standards to allow other languages to be incorporated.  This is
> obsolete in ANS'85.

In all honesty, I didn't know that the ENTER verb was *ever* a part of
standard COBOL; I'd always assumed it was a Burroughs Medium System
extension to ANSI-68 COBOL that the compilers for Burroughs' other platforms
elected (IMHO wisely!) not to emulate!

The '85 standard does mark ENTER as obsolete, and both the '85 and '74
standards indicate that the implementor of a compliant compiler is free to
omit support for the ENTER verb without impact on the compiler's compliance
with the standard (IMHO a rather odd thing to do!).

> Note that other forms of the ENTER also existed

Only one, so far as I know, in the standards:  "ENTER language-name
[routine-name]".

> and were replaced by  CALL and the extension ENTRY.

CALL, yes; but since ENTRY isn't standard COBOL and ENTER was (again, much
to my surprise and shock!), I can't agree that ENTER replaces ENTRY in
standard COBOL of whatever vintage.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote 

> In all honesty, I didn't know that the ENTER verb was *ever* a part of
> standard COBOL; I'd always assumed it was a Burroughs Medium System
> extension to ANSI-68 COBOL that the compilers for Burroughs' other platforms
> elected (IMHO wisely!) not to emulate!

It certainly was part of ANS'68, and probably was in previous
standards, I recall (only just) using it prior to having an ANS'68
compiler where it was used to call the disk access routines.
 
> > Note that other forms of the ENTER also existed
> 
> Only one, so far as I know, in the standards:  "ENTER language-name
> [routine-name]".

> CALL, yes; but since ENTRY isn't standard COBOL and ENTER was (again, much
> to my surprise and shock!), I can't agree that ENTER replaces ENTRY in
> standard COBOL of whatever vintage.

I should have been clearer, other forms of ENTER did exist beyond
those in the standard, for example ENTER LINKAGE, and IBM had some
form of ENTER RETURN or similar which was replaced by GOBACK.

And I was specific that ENTRY was an extension.
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote

> CALL, yes; but since ENTRY isn't standard COBOL and ENTER was (again, much
> to my surprise and shock!), I can't agree that ENTER replaces ENTRY in
> standard COBOL of whatever vintage.

I have found the reference that indicated that a (non-standard) form
of ENTER had been used for what is now done with the (non-standard)
ENTRY.

In P.B.Robinson's 'Advanced Cobol : ANS 74' (1976) he has:

"""Implementors also introduced variations and expansions of the
statement, such as:
     ENTER LINKAGE
     ENTER entry-name
to allow, for example, for multiple entry points."""

This book also has some amusing quotes in 'Appendix 6: Goodies to
come' which discusses JOD proposals:

"""10. Functions to be introduced, ...
PL/1 has tried to amalgamate all the high-level languages - but who,
outside the interested propagandists, talks about PL/1 nowadays?"""

"""14. Proposal to delete the ALPHABETIC usage: high time - what use
is it, anyway?"""

"""25. Structured programming looms large (despite ists small stature
as a real subject)."""
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: In answer to RW - again (was: Sorts (revised)

_(reply depth: 39)_

- **From:** robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2004-07-17T00:01:48+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<40f86beb.445957493@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <40f08a0f.180824052@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407111102.59a80371@posting.google.com> <40f1aef9.4304718@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407121258.8a48ced@posting.google.com> <40f398d0.129723189@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407131151.39465d2c@posting.google.com> <40f46c5f.183890458@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407141234.114d71a2@posting.google.com> <40f627c4.297415925@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407151830.53119503@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>> I've not seen applications do database administration, but it is
>> hypothetically possible.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>relied on to be users.

Fortune 100 companies do not run on single machines.
I use tools such as TOAD Xpert to administer databases. I don't embed
administration in application programs.

>> >It seems to work very well as it does currently, what _advantage_
>> >would there be in your suggestion ?
>> 
>> The interaction between compiler and database would be defined by an external
>> Standard rather than being ad hoc defined by the database provider. This
would
>> make source code portable.
>
>Which _particular_ parts that are not currently 'source code portable'
>would be improved by defining how the pre-processor and compiler
>interract ?

I posted an example yesterday -- an embedded PL/SQL program that fell over
trying to dereference a null pointer. If Cobol  and SQL compilers had been
better integrated, I tend to think (but cannot 'prove') that wouldn't have
happened. 

>In fact what part does 'interraction' play _at_all_ in the source code?

It's about responsibility. If the Cobol compiler just compiles defective code
presented to it, the Cobol folks tell you to take your complaint to the
precompiler folks .. who point the finger back at the Cobol compiler. The game
is called Problem Evasion And Finger Pointing.

On the other hand, when bad code is issue of an Integrated compiler, there is a
greater probability the Cobol and SQL parents will work together to fix it.

>> Again, portability and external controls.
>
>Be specific, what is non-portable now that this interract would change
>?  Which lines of source code would be different ?

I'm talking top-down generalities and you're asking for bottom-up specifics. 

Ok, here's one, although relatively minor. Source programs are written in Free
Form. The Oracle precompiler doesn't understand the compiler's free form option;
it wants its own option. 

>> In the past, language standards lived on islands that didn't
>> communicate with other islands. 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>obsolete in ANS'85.

I know. Mr. Stevens thought it specific to Burroughs but IBM used it as well. In
fact, that's where the idea came from. The difference was that Burroughs gave
the assembly language program access to Cobol data names, whereas IBM did not.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> It's about responsibility. If the Cobol compiler just compiles defective code
> presented to it, the Cobol folks tell you to take your complaint to the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> a greater probability the Cobol and SQL parents will work together to fix it.

What that would seem to require is that the Cobol standard defines
SQL, or perhaps the SQL standard defines Cobol.  Or perhaps only a
defined subset is used, as in Fujitsu.

The only way that Cobol can 'work together' is if the compiler
understands SQL.  As each SQL vendor has their own extensions this
means that each compiler must either use a subset of all of these or
ignore the different bits.  Either way the result is unlikely to be an
improvement over what currently happens.

> >Be specific, what is non-portable now that this interract would change
> >?  Which lines of source code would be different ?
> 
> I'm talking top-down generalities and you're asking for bottom-up specifics. 

Exactly. Your top-down generalities are useless without understanding
and solving the bottom-up specifics.


> I know. Mr. Stevens thought it specific to Burroughs but IBM used it as well.
> In fact, that's where the idea came from. 

It seems to me that it 'came from' COBOL, being part of the earliest
standards.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
>
>> It's about responsibility. If the Cobol compiler just compiles defective code
>> presented to it, the Cobol folks tell you to take your complaint to the
>> precompiler folks .. who point the finger back at the Cobol compiler. The
game
>> is called Problem Evasion And Finger Pointing.
>> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>defined subset is used, as in Fujitsu.

The SQL Standard devotes a whole section (#5) to host language "Bindings".

"Description: This part of ISO/IEC 9075 specifies syntax for embedding
SQL-statements in a compilation unit that otherwise conforms to the standard for
a particular programming language (host language); How an equivalent compilation
unit may be derived that conforms to the particular programming language
standard. In that equivalent compilation unit, each embedded SQL-statement has
been replaced by one or more statements in the host language, some of which
invoke an SQL externally-invoked procedure that, when executed, has an effect
equivalent to executing the SQL-statement; Syntax for direct invocation of
SQL-statements,and; SQL language to support dynamic preparation and execution of
SQL-statements."

You can download a copy for $18 from:
http://www.techstreet.com/cgi-bin/detail?product_id=229081

>The only way that Cobol can 'work together' is if the compiler
>understands SQL.  As each SQL vendor has their own extensions this
>means that each compiler must either use a subset of all of these or
>ignore the different bits.  Either way the result is unlikely to be an
>improvement over what currently happens.

You wouldn't say they if you ever used Oracle's Pro*Cobol precompiler. Anything
would be an improvement.

In my humble opinion, there are only three dominant commercial databases:
Oracle, mainframe DB2 and SQL Server (formerly Sybase). IBM supports its own
extensions, so compiler companies would have only two to worry about.

>> I know. Mr. Stevens thought it specific to Burroughs but IBM used it as well.
>> In fact, that's where the idea came from. 
>
>It seems to me that it 'came from' COBOL, being part of the earliest
>standards.

The earliest Cobol Standard was in 1968. CODASYL was not a standards body.
Before 1968, there were many more incompatibilities between brands than we have
today. Some manufacturers, such as Honeywell and RCA, regarded IBM as a de-facto
standard and copied its extensions. 

I'd seen ENTER ASSEMBLY LANGUAGE used in IBM 7090/7040 Cobol programs written in
the early '60s. I didn't use it because it was feature poor compared to the
assembler. I wrote called programs. 

The Burroughs implementation was better. I used it occasionally for features
such as Translate, which was missing from that Cobol.
```

```
wants to access, they would be restricting their market by limiting
this in any way.
```

```
Just when did IBM 'discontinue their precompiler' ?

Just because they may have produced an integrated compiler with SQL

> MySQL is a toy, akin to indexed files from another era.

can choose from, ones that I don't have to throw 6 figure numbers at
yet can outperform those.  Ones that don't rely on heavy handed
marketing, but on getting the job done reliably without high overheads
in administration.

Apart from MySQL and PostgreSQL the best include SQLlite for embedded
systems through Borland's Interbase and its spin off Firebird, to

Enterprise'.

Mainframe programming sites are not part of ANSI or ISO yet they
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> MySQL is a toy, akin to indexed files from another era. 

Your apparent criticism is rather strange, ironic even when, you say:

> In my humble opinion, the best database is Informix. 

Perhaps you are completely unaware that Informix _is_ 'indexed files
from another era'.  Informix was based entirely on using their C-ISAM
files as the underlying structure.  The very same C-ISAM files as is
used by MF in OCDS for indexed sequential, the same as was used by MF
as the basis of the Level II format in the early 80s.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >Why do you think that IBM view this as 'unnecessary' ?  Do they think
> >that PC clients using MF Cobol are 'unnecessary' ?  Or indeed that
> >users of any language running on PC are 'unnecessary' ?
 
> It is obvious that IBM compilers share a common front-end. Enterprise Cobol for
> zOS and Cobol Set for AIX already have integrated SQL; VisualAge for Windows is
> very likely to have it on the next release (it already has integrated CICS). How
> long will IBM support other Cobol compilers?

They are not 'supporting other compilers', they are supporting use of
their DB2 database.
 
> On that topic, I looked at the four leading Open Source databases and found only
> MaxDB has precompilers for any language, 

That certainly is _not_ true (yet again).  I use ecpg which is
PostgreSQL's preprocessor for C/C++ (it is listed on the page you
referenced).  Perl, Python, PHP do not require pre-processing as they
use DB style accessing which is standardised for all.

And, of course, anything that accesses ODBC, or unixODBC, can use
MySQL, PostgreSQL, or many others.  This includes Fujitsu NetCobol for
Linux, MicroFocus, CobolScript, Ruby, and many you maynot have heard
of.

> http://www.microfocus.com/mfnewsletter/20040701_008.asp

> and Cobol isn't one of them. What are we Cobol programmers to do?

Use Fujitsu ?

Use CLI/ODBC ?

"""CLI/ODBC is an SQL application programming interface that can be
called by your database applications. It passes dynamic SQL statements
as database function calls. Unlike embedded SQL it does not require
host variables or a precompiler."""

Use PostISAM (if/when developed)?

""PostISAM is an ISAM wrapper for libpq that generates SQL statements
on the fly from traditional ISAM (read, write, start) statements.
PostgreSQL could then be used as a file system for legacy COBOL
applications.

PostISAM is written in STL and is designed to be portable to different
COBOL compilers. Unlike Embedded SQL, PostISAM will allow PostgreSQL
to be a drop in replacement for a traditional ISAM file system.

PostISAM can be used with any compiler that:
1. Can call C routines in external libraries
2. Use a user provided ISAM file system (most commercial COBOL
implementations allow this)."""
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> >Which _particular_ parts that are not currently 'source code portable'
> >would be improved by defining how the pre-processor and compiler
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> happened. 

You probaly wouldn't see the irony in your example, not even if you
usually noticed irony.

You are asking for a _standard_ way of interacting to improve
portability when your problem was entirely related to use of
_non_standard_ facilities that are _non_portable_and _abused_ those by
ignoring what the manual tells you.

  ADDRESS OF is _NOT_ part of the Cobol standard (that you are using).

  Referencing linkage section items not passed by the CALL is 
         _specifically_disallowed_ by the standard.

Granted your refernce to it was merely to get the address passed, but
that is still a reference.  There is also no guarantee that you will
get a NULL return.

The 'bad code' was not a result of the pre-processor at all, but was
the result of your non-standard and non-portable code.

So why are you asking for _more_ standards, when you flagrantly ignore
the ones that you already have.  But then you never seem to know what
the standard says and what is, or is not, part of it.
```

```
>I think that once you see this implemented in

>correctly.

I don't think BASED would make any difference. The problem occurs in PL/SQL
programs shipped to the server. Precompiler generated code must replace
references to Cobol host variables with the value of those variables. If they
don't exist, it should replace the value with a null string. The Oracle
precompiler doesn't do that now, so I doubt it would act differently under
BASED.
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:40fb3bbd.18924056@news.optonline.net...
> "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
Snip>
> >I think that once you see this implemented in
> >more compilers (BASED phrase) you will see that SQL processors will handle it
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't understand a precompiler that

"must replace references to Cobol host variables with the value of those
variables"

I have only worked with a few precompilers, but I haven't EVER seen it do that.
(I have lost the origin of this part of the current thread, so I don't know the
exact code or if you were running on supported software to whose vendor you
reported the problem).

The precompilers that I know of do not look at the VALUE of host-variables, but
rather to their "location".  In the case of ANY *LINKAGE SECTION* item, the
exact "virtual address" canNOT be determined at precompile time.  In fact, if
this were true, a subprogram referencing host-variables could ONLY be called by
a single program.  This has nothing to do with whether the Linkage Section
host-variable has its address "set" by referencing data in the calling program
(BY CONTENT or BY REFERENCE) or by an explicit SET ADDRESS OF statement within
the Procedure Division of the called subprogram.

Robert,
  Did the "environment" where you had this problem support OTHER types of
Linkage Section items as Host Variables?  If so, I really do not understand the
problem (and would certainly communicate with both the compiler and precompiler
vendors to determine if this is an "acknowledged bug" or not.)

Again, as I wasn't able to find the specifications of the original program, I
may be misunderstanding exactly what the problem was, but from this part of the
thread something seems odd to me.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: SQL and precompilers (was: In answer to RW - again (was: Sorts (revised)

_(reply depth: 47)_

- **From:** robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2004-07-20T01:14:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<40fc6f66.27744636@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407111102.59a80371@posting.google.com> <40f1aef9.4304718@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407121258.8a48ced@posting.google.com> <40f398d0.129723189@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407131151.39465d2c@posting.google.com> <40f46c5f.183890458@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407141234.114d71a2@posting.google.com> <40f627c4.297415925@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407151830.53119503@posting.google.com> <40f86beb.445957493@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407171210.25c7816b@posting.google.com> <40f9ec81.544426227@news.optonline.net> <rqmKc.6836$Qu5.534@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40fa5f2d.573786842@news.optonline.net> <CKAKc.7425$Qu5.4373@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40fb3bbd.18924056@news.optonline.net> <0YWKc.7477$mL5.6464@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:40fb3bbd.18924056@news.optonline.net...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> >more compilers (BASED phrase) you will see that SQL processors will handle
it
>> >correctly.
>>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>reported the problem).

The PL/SQL statement said:

if :COBOL-INDICATOR = 'N'  then
  PLSQL_VARIABLE :=  ANOTHER_PLSQL_VARIABLE;
else
  PLSQL_VARIABLE := :OPTIONAL-COBOL-VARIABLE;
end if;

The indicator contained 'N' and the pointer to OPTIONAL-COBOL-VARIABLE was null.

In the preprocessor output, the inline PL/SQL program had been moved to a big
literal in working-storage. References to Cobol host variables had been replaced
by generated names such as ":b1", which did not exist in the intermediate file. 

This implies the generated code is doing an EXECUTE IMMEDIATE on the big
literal. Before that execution, it is getting the addresses of the Cobol
variables (with SQLADDR) and storing them in a generated structure. Their
position in the structure must somehow relate them to the undefined "b1". 

Here is the problem: the target of EXECUTE IMMEDIATE (as with PREPARE) is not
permitted to refer to variables outside the block of code. One must replace the
placekeeper :b1 with a value before issuing the EXECUTE IMMEDIATE. Oracle must
have a client-side 'editor' function doing that. The program is abending during
that editing, even though the optional variable would not be referenced at
execution time. 

If the pointer were changed from a parameter pointer to a BASED data-pointer, it
would fail the same way .. because in both cases the structure would contain a
null pointer. The editor doesn't know nor care where the pointer came from.

The fix is simple. In the editor, if a pointer is null, put a null string into
the source code. Don't cause an abend by trying to use it. The error is not in
the precompiler, it's in the editor.

>The precompilers that I know of do not look at the VALUE of host-variables, but
>rather to their "location".  In the case of ANY *LINKAGE SECTION* item, the
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>the Procedure Division of the called subprogram.

As I said, host variables are not permitted in a block that will be PREPAREd or
EXECUTEd IMMEDIATE. Why? Because in the old days, they were compiled and
executed on the server, which did not have access to the client's memory.

You may have seen shops where the in-house standard said _every_ SQL statement
must be PREPAREd. It is a real PITA having SQL in working-storage and the
corresponding host variables in procedure division FROM and INTO clauses. It
makes maintenance error-prone. The reason behind this is servers used to be much
faster than desktop machines, which made execution on the server faster than
execution on the client. Someone discovered that by running a timing test, then
created a shop standard that's being followed today, long after the technical
reason for it became obsolete .. and everyone forgot WHY the standard was
created. 

It's not the first or last instance of shop standards that no longer make sense.

>Robert,
>  Did the "environment" where you had this problem support OTHER types of
>Linkage Section items as Host Variables?  

Yes, they are used routinely.

>If so, I really do not understand the
>problem (and would certainly communicate with both the compiler and precompiler
>vendors to determine if this is an "acknowledged bug" or not.)

I hope you understand the problem now. I doubt Oracle support people would. 

The broken code had been promoted to QA and was causing previously 'certified'
programs to abend. I had one hour to fix it .. not enough time to communicate
with notoriously slow Oracle support. I fixed it by changing
:OPTIONAL-COBOL-VARIABLE to a working-storage item that has a valid address,
even though it would not be used at execution time.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> >You probaly wouldn't see the irony in your example, not even if you
> >usually noticed irony.

> >Granted your refernce to it was merely to get the address passed, but
> >that is still a reference.  There is also no guarantee that you will
> >get a NULL return.
 
> Do you now object to extensions to the Standard? 

Not at all.  But it is you that wants additional areas to be standard
for 'portabilty' when you obviously don't bother with portability or
keeping within standards yourself.

> At any rate, I didn't reference the missing
> data, the precompiler generated code to reference it.

You told the compiler to do this.

If you were actually concerned about standards in any way you would
know that your problem could never have occurred with ANSI SQL because
the host variables must be declared inside a BEGIN DECLARE in
Working-Storage.  eg:

""" ---------------------------------
Host variables are defined by putting them into a declaration section.
According to the IBM SAA and ANSI embedded SQL standards, host
variables are defined by surrounding the normal variable declarations
with the following:

EXEC SQL BEGIN DECLARE SECTION END-EXEC
/* variable declarations */
EXEC SQL END DECLARE SECTION END-EXEC
 --------------------- """

If you had used this _standard_ mechanism your code would have been
portable and would not have failed because you would have moved the
parameter to these as your 'fix' later did.

> If I had used a 2002-compliant compiler and written conforming code, the same
> error would have occurred. 

If you had used ANSI SQL standard it would not have.

It seems that your complaint was based on lack of understanding and
lack of use of the standards provided, your request for a 'fix' that
would integrate the compiler and SQL more closely by providing a
standard is an effort to _blame_ someone else (ie some standards
committee) eg:

>> If Cobol  and SQL compilers had been better integrated, I tend to
think
>> (but cannot 'prove') that wouldn't have happened. 

and:

>> It's about responsibility. If the Cobol compiler just compiles
defective
>> code presented to it, the Cobol folks tell you to take your
complaint to
>> the precompiler folks .. who point the finger back at the Cobol
compiler.
>> The game is called Problem Evasion And Finger Pointing.

You had written non-[ANSI-]standard, non-portable, defective code and
you want to do "Finger Pointing" to the compilers, the pre-compilers
_and_ the standards committees, and won't take responsibility for
learning and using the standards that _are_ provided.
```

```
No, you told the program to dereference at run-time.  You didn't
realise this, but the EXECUTE causes the whole SQL to be sent to the
server, first it processes _all_ the host variable references.  The
'if flag = ' is not Cobol, it is evaluated by the server, _not_ the
Cobol run-unit, well after the host variable has been accessed -
causing the abend.

> earlier. 

No, that is not true of either database. 

Oracle had made these sections optional, you can use BEGIN DECLARE and
can limit the scope of host variables with DECLARE_SECTION=YES or
MODE=ANSI.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>And for ANSI conformance.  MODE=ANSI sets DECLARE_SETION=YES.
>

It would take five minutes to write a sed or perl 'pre-precompiler' to insert
BEGIN DECLARE at the beginning of working-storage and END DECLARE just before
procedure division. Oracle says they did it for IBM compatibility; I believe
them.
-------------------------------------------------
 
A little research reveals that MaxDB/SAPDB is a rebadged ADABAS (sans Natural),
developed by Software AG (the SAP folks) in the '70s for use on IBM mainframes
and since ported to many platforms  .. without success. It's ironic that it no
longer has a Cobol precompiler, since that's the environment where it was born.

It is unsuited to large tables because it insists on sucking the whole table
into memory, which makes it fast but unscalable. 

Involved parties are Texas A&M, long out of the picture, and Computer
Associates, who appear to be a marketing agent milking it for cash, as is their
wont.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MaxDB = SAPDB = ADABAS (was: Sorts (revised)

_(reply depth: 48)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2004-07-21T00:45:37-07:00

```
Oracle _ADDED_ BEGIN DECLARE to be compatible with ANSI and IBM, who
had added it earlier, but Oracle left it optional depending on flags.

> A little research reveals that MaxDB/SAPDB is a rebadged ADABAS (sans Natural),
> developed by Software AG (the SAP folks) 

Software AG and SAP are completely different companies.

> in the '70s for use on IBM mainframes

with Fujitsu or a ODBC pre-compiler, such as is available for MF.
 
> It is unsuited to large tables because it insists on sucking the whole table
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >Which is really strange because DB2 still uses BEGIN DECLARE and I see
> >no evidence that this is optional at all.
> 
> Here is evidence on AS/400. 
> http://publib.boulder.ibm.com/html/as400/v4r5/ic2924/index.htm?info/db2/rbafzmst69.htm

I am not sure how that supports your view:

"""SQL declare sections should be specified for host languages, other
than RPG and REXX, so that the source program conforms to the IBM SQL
standard of SQL. SQL declare sections are required for all host
variables in C++. The SQL declare section should appear before the
first reference to the variable. Host variables are declared without
the use of these statements in RPG, and they are not declared at all
in REXX."""

This states they should be used except they are not used in RPG and
REXX.  They are required in C++.  It doesn't mention Cobol.  It
doesn't mention 'optional'.

You will note that the 'IBM SQL standard' does require this, so if
Oracle wants to be compatible with 'IBM SQL' it should too.

> >In fact it your: "Oracle dropped that in version 8 to be compatible
> >with IBM, which dropped it earlier." is completely the reverse.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

Not 'recently', no.  As far as I can tell it was not part of early SQL
pre-compilers and was added with ANSI.  The point is it was not
'dropped' as you claimed, as it is still part of the pre-compiler.

> >> [MaxDB] is unsuited to large tables because it insists on sucking the whole 
> >> table into memory, which makes it fast but unscalable. 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> installed to support that application.

The 'current SAP product' will run on a range of RDBMS systems,
including Oracle, CA's Ingres, DB/2, MS SQL, Adabas-D and SAP DB.  SAP
[re]sell these to suit the customer's preferences. There is _no_ "the
one".

Adabas-D became the SAP RDBMS in the early 90s and was renamed SAP DB
in 1997.

Given they claim users with several hundred GigaBytes, even on Windows
servers, I very much doubt your claim that it "insists on sucking the
whole table into memory".  Especially when they claim 'unlimited
database size'.

It may well be that _some_ smaller frequently used tables can be
specified as being memory resident to improve performance, but this
would have no effect on the suitability of the database for large
tables that are not so marked.
 

> >You just make this stuff up.  CA has Ingress and will soon be
> >releasing Ingress II.  It does have a connection with SAP's DB, but
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> You are correct; he was mistaken; I should have checked.

It may well be that R3 does not work well on CA's Ingres which SAP had
bought (one copy of) to resell to a customer that specified it.

'Dumping' into the Open Source market is to give it a much wider
visibility and acceptance.  Fujitsu is supporting PostgreSQL
financially and with code.  CA is putting APE (Ingress II) into the
market. Firebird is there already.

The point is that either they have to spend millions continuing to
develop it as a proprietry system or they put it out as OpenSource and
get the community to develop it for them (with support).  That is the
new market.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >"""... so that the source program conforms to the IBM SQL
> >standard of SQL. ..."""

> IBM isn't a standards body. The author is referring to the ANSI standard.

No. It quite clearly says 'IBM SQL standard' which is likely to be
compatible with ANSI but more stringent in some areas, or perhaps a
subset in others.

It is ironic that you complain about 'not being ANSI' when you
frequently claim 'industry standards' as being superior, such as the
way you use pointers.

> The paragraph before describes how IBM Cobol precompilers actually work.
> Oracle now works the same way.

Neither 'dropped' BEGIN DECLARE.

> "If SQL declare sections are specified in the program, only the variables
> declared within the SQL declare sections can be used as host variables. If SQL
> declare sections are not specified in the program, all variables in the program
> are eligible for use as host variables. "

Yes, but there the pre-compiler converts data types with DECLARE
sections.  If you don't use this then it converts every relevant item:

"""If the Oracle directive DECLARE_SECTION=NO is set (the default),
Oracle converts all COMP, BINARY or COMP-4 data items to COMP-5."""

Potentially your record layouts, linkage items and tables which are
not used as host variables could be 'modified' with strange results.
```

_(reply depth: 53)_

- **From:** robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2004-07-22T23:35:43+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4100351f.90175152@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407181235.56f566b9@posting.google.com> <40fc71f0.28394570@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407192241.5c888040@posting.google.com> <40fce5bc.58042712@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407201132.24404309@posting.google.com> <40fdbf44.113740115@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407202345.72ec2a23@posting.google.com> <40fee325.3640108@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407211919.5baa40c6@posting.google.com> <40ff7514.41004627@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407221126.7f11a35b@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
[snipped ad hominem ad nauseum]
>> The paragraph before describes how IBM Cobol precompilers actually work.
>> Oracle now works the same way.
>
>Neither 'dropped' BEGIN DECLARE.

Right, they made it optional.

>> "If SQL declare sections are specified in the program, only the variables
>> declared within the SQL declare sections can be used as host variables. If
SQL
>> declare sections are not specified in the program, all variables in the
program
>> are eligible for use as host variables. "
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>not used as host variables could be 'modified' with strange results.

You misunderstand what they mean by "convert".  The precompiler doesn't convert
your data division at compile time, it "converts" (casts would be a better word)
host variables from Cobol types to SQL types  _during execution_. 

For example, if you said SELECT whatever FROM CUSTOMER WHERE CUSTOMER_NUMBER =
:a-BINARY-variable, it would cast a-BINARY-variable from comp-5 to NUMBER when
it executes the statement. That would be a problem if BINARY meant something
different from comp-5. It probably wouldn't find the customer. Similarly, it
would cast outputs with type NUMBER into comp-5. If BINARY meant something else,
your arithmetic wouldn't work right.

If you set DECLARE_SECTION=YES, what would it do differently? It would cast
NUMBER to the lowest common denominator: a temporary variable with display
number type (pic 9). Then it would say 'move a-BINARY-variable to
temporary-variable. SELECT ... WHERE CUSTOMER_NUMBER = :temporary-variable'.
The double conversion is a waste of time in most environments.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> >Neither 'dropped' BEGIN DECLARE.
> 
> Right, they made it optional.

Thank you.
 
> >Yes, but there the pre-compiler converts data types with DECLARE
> >sections.  If you don't use this then it converts every relevant item:
> >
> >"""If the Oracle directive DECLARE_SECTION=NO is set (the default),
> >Oracle converts all COMP, BINARY or COMP-4 data items to COMP-5."""
 
> You misunderstand what they mean by "convert".  The precompiler doesn't convert
> your data division at compile time, it "converts" (casts would be a better word)
> host variables from Cobol types to SQL types  _during execution_. 

A 'cast' is not a convertion at all, as you say.  So, Oracle are
_wrong_ when they use the word 'convert' and RW knows better.

It seems to me that the pre-compiler _does_ convert data types as it
claims.  For example here:

"""As Oracle can produce code with either COMP or COMP-5 variables, it
has two sets of copyfiles. The standard sqlca.cob, oraca.cob and
sqlda.cob all have COMP data items. The sqlca5.cob, oraca5.cob and
sqlda5.cob files have COMP-5 data items. If you are using the
comp5=yes Oracle directive, you must set the COBSQL directive MAKESYN
to convert the COMP items in the SQLCA to COMP-5."""

This is specifically saying that it converts the _code_ in a
_source_file_ to change the COMP items to COMP-5.
 
> For example, if you said SELECT whatever FROM CUSTOMER WHERE CUSTOMER_NUMBER =
> :a-BINARY-variable, it would cast a-BINARY-variable from comp-5 to NUMBER when
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> else, your arithmetic wouldn't work right.

And would it also be true to say: if COMP-4 is not the same as COMP-5
then "it probably wouldn't find the customer" or "your arithmetic
wouldnt work right" ?

> If you set DECLARE_SECTION=YES, what would it do differently? 

It would _only_ do the _conversion_ between the BEGIN DECLARE and END
DECLARE where it can be specifically contained to only convert the
host variables rather than in every data item (of the specified types)
in the whole program.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>_source_file_ to change the COMP items to COMP-5.

The quotation is confusing. First it says to use the sqlca5.cob, which contains
comp-5, then it says to use the MAKESYN directive to convert comp to comp-5
therein. Why is MAKESYN required if they're already comp-5?

Note that SQLCA is INCLUDEd by the precompiler, not COPYd by the compiler, so
the precompiler would be capable of changing it. 

Another manual is clearer, and supports your contention:

"An option comp5 is also present in the PCCCOB.CFG file. 

If the option comp5=yes: 

All COMP data items (if they are potential host variables) are converted to
COMP-5. 
All data items generated by the precompiler will be declared as COMP-5. 

If comp5=no: 

The precompiler ignores COMP-5 host variables. 
Precompiled files generally do not run on Intel platforms. "
http://www-rohan.sdsu.edu/doc/oracle/server803/A53715_01/ch2.htm

Yet another:

"MAKESYN Converts all COMP host variables to COMP-5 host variables. The default
situation, if MAKESYN is not set, is that all variables (not just host
variables) are converted from COMP to COMP-5. 

NOMAKESYN No conversion of variables or host variables from COMP to COMP-5 is
carried out. "
http://www.emunix.emich.edu/info/cobol/books/dbcsql.htm

Ok, you were correct .. about COMP.

>And would it also be true to say: if COMP-4 is not the same as COMP-5
>then "it probably wouldn't find the customer" or "your arithmetic
>wouldnt work right" ?

The quotations do not mention BINARY or COMP-4.

Which compiler treats COMP-4 as different from COMP-5? Excepting Unisys, the
only one I know of was Realia, where COMP-4 guaranteed small-endian. I believe
all compilers treat BINARY the same as COMP-5 i.e. the machine's native integer

>> If you set DECLARE_SECTION=YES, what would it do differently? 
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>in the whole program.

I believe you are correct. I've worked on programs that took the default
DECLARE_SECTION=NO but did contain BEGIN/END DECLARE. In cases where they
inadvertantly used host variables outside the BEGIN/END block, there was no
error reported. The precompiler appeared to treat BEGIN and END as comments.
```

```
> Which compiler treats COMP-4 as different from COMP-5? Excepting Unisys, the
> only one I know of was Realia, where COMP-4 guaranteed small-endian. I believe
> all compilers treat BINARY the same as COMP-5 i.e. the machine's native
integer
>

IBM mainframe:
   COMP-4 = Binary (sensitive to TRUNC compiler option)
   COMP-5 = Binary with TRUNC(OPT)

IBM PC
   COMP-4 = Binary = big-endian
   COMP-5 = small-endian

IBM OS/400
   no COMP-5 support (last time I looked, may have changed recently)

Micro Focus
   (default on PC)
    COMP-4 = Binary = big-endian (with default of IBM-COMP, if I recall
correctly)
    COMP-5 = small-endian (I can't remember but it may also be NOIBM-COMP)

Fujitsu,
  Current LRM does not show ANY support for COMP-4
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> >Microfocus - on PC COMP-4 is bigendian, COMP-5 is littleendian.
> 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Littleendian machines.

You are confused.  I had seen previously that you called PCs
bigendian, but I can't pick you up on _every_ error you make.

PCs, in fact all Intel x86 (and AMD, Cyrix etc) chips  since the 8008,
are littleendian.

In many compilers, such as MicroFocus and Fujitsu, COMP-5 is 'native',
thus on PCs and other x86 based machines, eg SCO Unix, Linux, COMP-5
is littleendian.

COMP-4, where supported, is generally bigendian.

I think that you are wrong about Realia.  It is probably that you have
big and little endian reversed in your mind.

You may want to know that:
"""" ---------------
Target compilers supported by DB2 that treat COMP, COMP-4, BINARY COMP
and COMP-5 as equivalent are:

    * IBM(R) COBOL Set for AIX(R)
    * Micro Focus COBOL for AIX
------------------------- """
AIX runs on IBM Power proceesors which are big endian when running
AIX.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>> The one used in at least one current SAP product. Most SAP shops have it
>> installed to support that application.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>one".

Yes there is. It's called Advanced Planner Optimizer (APO). It copies "master
data", i.e. all the data it needs, from R3's database into its own .. called
LiveCache aka SAP DB aka Adabas-D. It's called LiveCache because _all_ tables
are memory-resident (they're also on disk). You can read about it in this
document, with particular attention to pages 2 and 4. The last sentence on page
4 explicitly says it is SAP DB.

https://www.sdn.sap.com/content_documents/SAP%20APO%20with%20SAP%20liveCache%207.4.pdf

>Given they claim users with several hundred GigaBytes, even on Windows
>servers, I very much doubt your claim that it "insists on sucking the
>whole table into memory".  Especially when they claim 'unlimited
>database size'.

>It may well be that _some_ smaller frequently used tables can be
>specified as being memory resident to improve performance, but this
>would have no effect on the suitability of the database for large
>tables that are not so marked.

Right. That was an APO decision, not a requirement of SAP DB. They don't mark
_some_ tables, they mark _all_ tables .. typically in the 10GB range.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MaxDB = SAPDB = ADABAS (was: Sorts (revised)

_(reply depth: 52)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)

- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407181235.56f566b9@posting.google.com> <40fc71f0.28394570@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407192241.5c888040@posting.google.com> <40fce5bc.58042712@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407201132.24404309@posting.google.com> <40fdbf44.113740115@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407202345.72ec2a23@posting.google.com> <40fee325.3640108@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407211919.5baa40c6@posting.google.com> <41002fd1.88816488@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>>> A little research reveals that MaxDB/SAPDB is ... 
>>> It is unsuited to large tables because it insists on sucking the
whole
>>> table into memory, which makes it fast but unscalable. 

> >> The one used in at least one current SAP product. Most SAP shops have it
> >> installed to support that application.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >one".

> Yes there is. It's called Advanced Planner Optimizer (APO). 

Unfortunately your 'A little research' is frequently far too little.

SAP APO is a Supply Chain Management APPLICATION.  It is _NOT_ a
RDBMS, it is _NOT_ R3, it is a completely different thing for managing
supply chains when distributing products to warehouses and retailers.

SAP R3 runs on a range of RDBMS including Oracle, Sybase, MS SQL, ..
There is _NO_ "the one".  If there was a 'the one' it wouldn't be APO.

> It copies "master
> data", i.e. all the data it needs, from R3's database into its own .. called
> LiveCache aka SAP DB aka Adabas-D. It's called LiveCache because _all_ tables
> are memory-resident (they're also on disk). 

'liveCache' is _NOT_ 'aka SAP DB'.  LiveCache is entirely different
product that optionally operates _between_ SAP ADO and SAP DB (or
possibly other RDBMS). They even draw diagrams and you still can't
'get it'.

It _DOES_NOT_ say that it makes "_all_ tables memory-resident".  It
does not say that the whole of any table is read into memory.  It says
'the data required is transferred to [the cache] and kept persistent".

> You can read about it in this
> document, with particular attention to pages 2 and 4. 

So could you, because it seems that made up your mind about what it
says indepenently of the actual words used.  Perhaps it is just too
technical, try getting hold of a simpler document about it.

Geez, Robert, do you have to get _everything_ backwards.  Your claim
was that "SAP DB" is unsuited to large tables because it /insists/ on
sucking the /whole table/ into memory, .."

SAP DB does not 'insist' anything.
SAP DB does not 'suck the whole table into memory'
SAP liveCache is not SAP DB, it is an add-on to SAP APO
SAP liveCache does not 'suck the whole table into memory'

> The last sentence on page 4 explicitly says it is SAP DB.

NO IT DOES NOT.  SAP APO is _NOT_ even an RDBMS.  liveCache is _NOT_
an RDBMS, it is an add on that works between an RDBMS (which may be
SAP DB or other) and SAP APO.  In the last sentence of page 4 it
merely directs you read more about some SAP products.

> Right. That was an APO decision, not a requirement of SAP DB. They don't mark
> _some_ tables, they mark _all_ tables .. typically in the 10GB range.

All _required_data_ is available to be used in memory by liveCache. 
This is not 'tables'.  It is _NOT_ "an APO decision", it is a function
of the optional separate liveCache.

On all systems there are caches that will save disk accesses.  SAP's
liveCache is just an extension to normal caching mechanisms that
allows the cache to interract better with the applications and with
the database (which may or need not be SAP DB).
```

```
You seem to want to change what you had claimed and go off into Red
Herring land, in order to appear less wrong.  Your usual deflection
path of 'I meants'.

You had claimed that MaxDB/SAP DB/Adabas """is unsuited to large
tables because it insists on sucking the whole table into memory,
which makes it fast but unscalable."""

When asked which Adabas this claim related to (ie the one from the 70s
or Adabas-D/SAP DB) you replied: """//The one// used in at least one
current SAP product. Most SAP shops have it installed to support that
application."""

The only current SAP product that is in 'most SAP shops' is R3.  R3
will run on many RDBMSes so there is no "The one" RDBMS.
 
It may well be that the application ADO requires SAP DB.  Your claim
about SAP DB 'insisting on sucking the whole table into memory' is
still wrong.

It may well be that liveCache does read data into memory (but not
necessarily whole tables), but this is not SAP DB, it is an add on, a
different product.

Your claim that MaxDB is 'unsuited to large tables' is wrong.
Your claim that MaxDB is 'unscalable' is wrong.

Whether ADO requires SAP DB or not is irrelevant, even if it also
requires LiveCache, even if _LiveCache_ does read [some or lots of]
data into memory.

> ADO is the one SAP application that requires SAP DB. Readers can verify 
> it in the document I cited.

Actualy it doesn't say that at all, though it might be.  The document
does say "SAP LiveCache is _based_on_ a main memory extension of SAP
DB _technology_" and "For more information behind the SAP DB
technology visit ...".

It would appear that liveCache is only of use to SAP applications and
primarily to ADO.  This does not make it, or SAP DB, required.

If you thik that I was _actually_ wrong in something I said then
please do be specific and provide some evidence, rather than vaugely
pointing at a document that does not support your claims.
```

```
Decision-makers think C++ 'can do GUI' whereas Cobol cannot. They staff projects
and impose language selection based on that faulty assumption. 

I'm now working on a project where all Web reports are written in the 'COGNOS
language'. COGNOS isn't a report generator, it's a business intelligence (BI)
product. Management paid millions for it, then reduced it to a Web report
generator. Reports could just as well have been produced by Cobol using CGI.
Management doesn't understand that.  Our job should be educating them.
```

```
robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >No one requires _you_ to use any of these items or to work for sites
> >that require their use.
> 
> Economics might. I work on Unix platforms specifically to avoid the tyranny of
> mainframe  'programming standards'. 

Standards are for a reason.  It allows the code to survive for decades
while programmers come and go each year.

If they went through 'what C did in the 70s', then 'what Pascal did in
the 80s' and 'what C++ did in the 90s' they would now be rewriting
everything to 'what Java is now'.  This would require several times
the number of programmers to reinvent the systems every few years, to
no beneit _at_all_ for the business.

> In this environment, nobody seems  to care
> about style standards. Miraculously, we survive without them. 

Now you have claimed to have rewritten whole systems.  In what way
does your rewrite represent the previous system 'surviving' ?  You are
also advocating reimplementing to a new style, in what way is that the
current system 'surviving'.

It isn't about _you_ surviving, it is about the _business_ surviving,
they do that by ensuring their systems continue to work for the least
cost.  That means using standards.

> Mainframers won't believe it.

I am sure that they shake your head in wonderment at your attitudes,
yes.
 
> You're right. But I'm right too. 'Most' means that more than 50% of Cobol
> programmers were immersed in the IBM mainframe culture. Do you dispute that?

I only dispute its relevence.
 
> The Screen Section simplified text-mode Green Screens. 

IBM Mainframe green screens were driven completely differently.  The
whole architecture of CICS (and many similar products) is completely
different from micro based screen section.

> We've since gone beyond
> that to GUI screens. Cobol offers no support there.

Nor does C or C++ or Ada or many other languages, they do it using API
calls - just as Cobol does.

> Best I remember, it was Honeywell .. but might have been Burroughs. The compiler
> said IF NUMERIC 'should' be made against PIC 9. If you mde it against PIC X, you
> must accept the default substituion, which was S9(1). Thus, 'A' is numeric
> because it's a plus one with a row-12 overpunch, according to the rules of
> punched cards.

So what is your point ?  You seem to be blaming the compiler for you
not reading the manual.

> >This fails to match 'IF NUMERIC'.
> 
> Why? It tests whether foo contains the digits 0 through 9.

Well actually, if you had read the manual, or knew the standard, you
would know that IF NUMERIC allows operational signs too.
```

```
In article <40edfe41.13936324@news.optonline.net>,
Robert Wagner <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote:
>riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
>
>>robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

[snip]

>>Standards are for a reason.  It allows the code to survive for decades
>>while programmers come and go each year.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>whether new code should follow obsolete standards. 

Mr Wagner, this presents a difficulty.  One of the things which increases 
productivity in a shop - as you have noticed before - is familiarity of 
all the coders with a given set of standards for how code is supposed to 
be written; you were, as I recalled, quite pleased with the results of a 
standards you had imposed on a shop you'd managed.

The question becomes... how does one determine the point of diminishing 
returns for a shop's standard?  At what point does one say 'Yes, everyone 
knows where to find I-O routines and edit logic... but now we have to make 
things different and that's going to slow us down for (n) months'?

(After one determines such a thing the question then becomes one of 'How 
do I persuade people to accept a decrease in my shop's productivity for 
(n) months in order to fix something they have to be convinced is 
broken?'... but that's a political question and the one asked above is a 
mere metrical one.)

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: In answer to RW - again (was: Sorts (revised)

_(reply depth: 23)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-07-12T09:21:28-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ccudq8$2usk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <JxoDc.2193$lh4.1300@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40de96ca.156736337@news.optonline.net> <cbmm4q$jgb$1@panix5.panix.com> <40df23bf.192826650@news.optonline.net> <cc1r5v$1vc2$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <40e519ed.583588217@news.optonline.net> <UFBGc.7081$oD3.1017@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40eb48b2.293922576@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407070118.194054a4@posting.google.com> <40ebcd11.327814837@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407071117.59ad0072@posting.google.com> <40ec9718.379541379@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:40ec9718.379541379@news.optonline.net...

> >> move 'A' to foo
> >> if foo is numeric
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Best I remember, it was Honeywell .. but might have been Burroughs. The
compiler
> said IF NUMERIC 'should' be made against PIC 9. If you mde it against PIC
X, you
> must accept the default substituion, which was S9(1).

Such an implementation would, it seems to me, be in violation of ANSI
X3.23-1974 page II-43, 5.2.1.2, Class condition, second paragraph.

"The usage of the operand being tested must be described as display.  ...
The NUMERIC test cannot be used with an item whose data description
describes the item as alphabetic or as a group item composed of elementary
items whose data description indicates the presence of operational sign(s).
If the data description of the data item begin tested does not indicate the
presence of an operational sign [note:  I'd say this includes both PICTURE 9
and PICTURE X], the item being tested is determined to be numeric only if
the contents are numeric and an operational sign is not present. ..."

Other evidence leads me to believe that the '68 standard (I do not have a
copy) was worded similarly, and I can't find any difference in the '85
standard's wording in this area.

> Thus, 'A' is numeric
> because it's a plus one with a row-12 overpunch, according to the rules of
> punched cards.

'A' is also @C1@ in EBCDIC and @41@ in ASCII.  According to the way I read
the standard, the corresponding Hollerith code acceptable to a COBOL numeric
test of either Picture X or Picture 9 Usage Display would be a single punch
in row 1; the acceptable EBCDIC code, @F1@; and the acceptable ASCII code,
@31@ .

Note: the 2002 standard does allow NUMERIC tests against items of category
NUMERIC; it no longer limits such tests to USAGE DISPLAY.

       -Chuck Stevens
```

```
<snip>
>
> >Note: the 2002 standard does allow NUMERIC tests against items of category
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Not true.

The 2002 Standard *defines* the IF NUMERIC class test against a BINARY (or
COMPUTATIONAL - or PACKED-DECIMAL) field as *only* passing when the data
*matches* the PICTURE (and usage).   Therefore, an IF NUMERIC class test against
a BINARY-CHAR (new USAGE) field will (always?) pass as any bit combination
matches the USAGE (and there is NO picture clause), for a
     02 Num-Field  Pic S99  Binary.

field if it as an "algebraic" (using the term loosely) value of 100 - even if it
"fits" in the field, it would FAIL an IF NUMERIC class test.

Personally, I was never certain this was the right way for the definition to be
made, but it IS what the Standard says.  Of course any compiler that supports a
"NOTRUNC" compiler option (as most? many? do) is already running in NON-Standard
mode - and can do whatever it wants with the "new" IF NUMERIC class test in that
environment.  It only needs to "match" the Standard when "standard  conforming"
options are in effect.
```

```
Can a conforming program compare Num-Field to an out-of-range value?

If Num-Field less than 100 and greater than -100
   Perform Good-Number
End-If

Would an optimizer discard the following condition and make the Perform
unconditional?

If Num-Field not greater than 99 and not less than -99
   Perform Good-Number
End-If
```

```
A conforming compiler must allow the comparisons; it could (but is not required
to) allow its optimizer to make the loop unconditional WHEN running in "Standard
conforming mode".  (Again, many/most compilers have a NOTRUNC - or equivalent
option for which these statements are NOT true).

FYI,
   Also of semi-relevance are the new
     HIGHEST-ALGEBRAIC
        and
     LOWEST-ALGEBRAIC

intrinsic functions which you may want to look at in the 2002 Standard.
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-07-13T09:42:11-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cd13d3$1n24$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <JxoDc.2193$lh4.1300@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40de96ca.156736337@news.optonline.net> <cbmm4q$jgb$1@panix5.panix.com> <40df23bf.192826650@news.optonline.net> <cc1r5v$1vc2$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <40e519ed.583588217@news.optonline.net> <UFBGc.7081$oD3.1017@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40eb48b2.293922576@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407070118.194054a4@posting.google.com> <40ebcd11.327814837@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407071117.59ad0072@posting.google.com> <40ec9718.379541379@news.optonline.net> <ccudq8$2usk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <40f39e89.131187942@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:40f39e89.131187942@news.optonline.net...

> >Note: the 2002 standard does allow NUMERIC tests against items of
category
> >NUMERIC; it no longer limits such tests to USAGE DISPLAY.

> That must be about packed decimal. Everything is valid in binary.

No, I'm afraid you've got it wrong again, Mr. Wagner.

ISO/IEC 1989:2002 page 361, 13.16.58.1 USAGE clause General Format includes
the following "category numeric only" USAGES:  binary; binary-char
[signed/unsigned]; binary-short [signed/unsigned]; binary-long
[signed/unsigned]; binary-double [signed/unsigned]; computational;
float-short, float-long, float-extend, and packed-decimal.

The implementor defines the format and content of each of these USAGEs (Page
363, 13.16.58.2 USAGE clause General Rules 4, 6, 10, 11, 12 and 13).

Now to Page 132, 8.8.4.1.3.3, Class test, General rule 2a1b:  "If the usage
of the data item ... is not display or national, the condition is true if
the content of the data item ... consists entirely of a valid representation
for the usage and, if a PICTURE clause is specified, its numeric value is
within the range of values permitted for the PICTURE clause."

A USAGE BINARY PICTURE 9 item containing the value @40000000000D@ should
FAIL an IF NUMERIC test on two counts:  1) the value is negative and 2) its
magnitude that which can be contained in a PICTURE 9 item of any USAGE.
Likewise an item described USAGE BINARY PICTURE S9(11) and containing
@40174876E800@ is NOT NUMERIC because what it contains is -100,000,000,000,
again, outside the range permitted for the item (even though integer
magnitudes up to 549,755,813,887 can be physically represented in the word
structure and 11 digits is the maximum size that can be specified for a
single-precision integer).

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-07-12T16:39:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ccv7g6$eco$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<40d93f2a.149354897@news.optonline.net> <40ddf3d4.115012207@news.optonline.net> <JxoDc.2193$lh4.1300@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40de96ca.156736337@news.optonline.net> <cbmm4q$jgb$1@panix5.panix.com> <40df23bf.192826650@news.optonline.net> <cc1r5v$1vc2$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <40e519ed.583588217@news.optonline.net> <UFBGc.7081$oD3.1017@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net> <40eb48b2.293922576@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0407070118.194054a4@posting.google.com> <40ebcd11.327814837@news.optonline.net>`

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:40ebcd11.327814837@news.optonline.net...

> Between 1960 and 1983, most Cobol lived on IBM mainframes.

Oh, I think I'd dispute that.  I don't think IBM users did all that much
with COBOL until the introduction of the IBM System 360/40 in 1965, and even
then assembler for business applications was decidedly more popular.

In my case, in the IBM shop I started at in '67, they ran a whole lot of
1401 emulation on the 360/40 and a corresponding amount of 7080 emulation on
the 360/65, with all the new development being done in ALC.  They later
offloaded the 1401 emulation onto two genuine 1401's, upgraded the 360/40 to
a /50 and picked up a second /50; these two covered much of the DOS Release
23 work as well as OS/MFT.  The /65 continued running both 7080 emulation
and OS/MFT.   All the 360 "native code" was in ALC; I think they'd begun
investigating the use of COBOL around the time I left in '69, but certainly
none was in production by then.

In contrast, Flow-Matic arguably is the parent of COBOL, having been
developed by Grace Hopper's group for the Univac I and later Univac II, and
upon the completion of the first specification for COBOL in 1960, all
indications are that COBOL was widely accepted in that environment
(certainly by the time of the Univac III).

Burroughs saw enough market in COBOL to build a machine specifically to run
that language (the B2500/3500, introduced in 1966).  These (and their
successors) were *very* successful competitors for the 360/30 and 360/40
(and their successors), and assembler code was decidedly the exception
(although ENTER SYMBOLIC from COBOL was hardly unknown back then!).

And there's some evidence to suggest the *first* system on which a
full-scale COBOL compiler actually ran was a DEC PDP-1 (in 1960), though I
can't vouch for it personally.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert.deletethis@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:40ebcd11.327814837@news.optonline.net...
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>then assembler for business applications was decidedly more popular.

Between 1963 and 1966 I worked at ARCO (earlier Richfield Oil) where the primary
computer was IBM 7044. Code was 80% COBOL and 20% FORTRAN. It was that way when
I started, so they must have been writing COBOL for several years prior. There
was no application code written in assembler. The only assembly language was on
1401s, which were used as input and print spoolers.

>In my case, in the IBM shop I started at in '67, they ran a whole lot of
>1401 emulation on the 360/40 and a corresponding amount of 7080 emulation on
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>none was in production by then.

Prior to 1998, I never saw a mainframe shop of any brand that had significant
assembly languge in application code. It was usually limited to 'glue' and
systems-type functions. The only exception was one shop that ran one application
in RCA301 emulation on a Spectra. I rewrote that application in the first year.
In 1998 I worked on a major payroll system written 100% in assembly language. It
was replaced by PeopleSoft (which is written in COBOL).

Tight assembly language, probably written in the '60s, runs very FAST on a
modern mainframe. That payroll system could plow through a 1G master file and
compute 200K paychecks in less than 15 minutes. The first attempt on PeopleSoft
took longer than 12 hours. Speeding it up required partitioning the database
over 30 disks and using lots of parallel processing. Thirty high-end servers
were still cheaper than two monster mainframes.

>Burroughs saw enough market in COBOL to build a machine specifically to run
>that language (the B2500/3500, introduced in 1966).  These (and their
>successors) were *very* successful competitors for the 360/30 and 360/40
>(and their successors), and assembler code was decidedly the exception
>(although ENTER SYMBOLIC from COBOL was hardly unknown back then!).

Yes! I worked on several of them, loved the machine and its instruction set.
Most lines of COBOL were done with a single machine instruction. Executables
were less than 1/3 the size of generated code on other machines.

>And there's some evidence to suggest the *first* system on which a
>full-scale COBOL compiler actually ran was a DEC PDP-1 (in 1960), though I
>can't vouch for it personally.

I vaguely recall reading it was on an IBM 7090 in 1959. That might be wrong but
I know from experpience the 7090/7040 series had a production-quality COBOL
compiler by at least 1961.
```

```
Bottom posting

robert.deletethis@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote in message news:<40de96ca.156736337@news.optonline.net>...
> "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:
> 
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> information and ideas.

Robert

Assertiveness has its benefits, but can be overdone.  This is not an
employer/employee environment.  Whether or not it is true or was
justified, giving examples of personal disagreements in the work
environment and how you always came off best with other people
quitting or being sacked gives the impression that that may be what
you would like to happen here, which tends to put other people's backs
up even more.  I think, not being one myself, that the most effective
managers are those who can be assertive when the need arises, but
mostly persuade and suggest in a non-confrontational manner, asking
for consensus opinions with reasons then discussing the options.  A
really skilful manager probably nearly always avoids the need for
confrontation and doesn't make people resentful.

I think there is great merit in being able to discuss matters of
technique and style while not necessarily agreeing with each other. 
For example, in the matter of program efficiency and your sort
examples, I think it is highly desirable to know how to write programs
and systems efficiently at all levels from code level to system
management and which approach is most appropriate for a given
situation. Having said that, some techniques may never be appropriate,
apart perhaps from being a part of going through the learning process,
when I/we/they didn't know any better.

In my experience the regular contributors to this group have always
been willing to contribute information and ideas, when they are not
having a rant <Grin>.  Electronic communication is just another form
of communication, in this forum it is not significantly different from
communicating by round robin letter.

Sometimes when reading a response with which one disagrees or which is
offensive (sometimes it's hard to tell whether it is just a wind
up<banter>), it is best not to reply too quickly, but let the dust
settle first.

I think you have made some beneficial contributions to the group and
could make more, as of course have and can the other contributors.  A
least it makes us all think about the topics and reconsider our
opinions, even if we don't change them.

As others have already stated, Bill Klein really has made and
continues to make a considerable contribution to the use of COBOL and
its improvement, and really does know a lot about the subject.

Robert Jones
```
